Here are the key findings:\n\n## ABA Formal Opinion 512 - Generative AI Ethics Guidance\n\nOn July 29, 2024, the ABA Standing Committee on Ethics & Professional Responsibility issued Formal Opinion 512, Generative Artificial Intelligence Tools.(no content)The opinion offers a useful compilation of ethics guidance keyed to the Model Rules of Professional Conduct.\n\n## Key Areas Covered\n\nTopics addressed by Formal Opinion 512 include confidentiality, fees, candor toward the tribunal, and more. The opinion specifically addresses:\n\n- **Competence (Model Rule 1.1)**: This obligates lawyers to provide competent representation to clients and requires they exercise the \"legal knowledge, skill, thoroughness and preparation reasonably necessary for the representation.\"\n\n- **Confidentiality (Model Rule 1.6)**: A lawyer using GAI must be cognizant of the duty to keep confidential all information relating to the representation of a client, regardless of its source, unless the client gives informed consent.\n\n- **Candor to Tribunals**: Duties to the tribunal require lawyers, before submitting materials, to review GAI output, including analysis and citations to authority, and to correct errors, including misstatements of law and fact, any failure to include controlling legal authority, and any misleading arguments.\n\n## Court Rules Tracking Tool\n\nResponsible AI in Legal Services (RAILS) compiles and organizes court orders, rules, and guidelines related to AI in this court tracking tool.\n\n## State-Level Guidance\n\nIn addition to the ABA's Formal Opinion 512, some states and the D.C. Violations may result in sanctions including striking pleadings, economic sanctions, contempt, or dismissal.\n\n**Eastern District of Pennsylvania (Judge Pratter)**: Requires disclosure of any use of generative AI in the preparation of documents.\n\n**District of New Mexico**: Has established disclosure and certification requirements for generative AI, requiring identification of the specific AI tool used.\n\n**Northern District of California (Judge Koh)**: Requires documents created with AI to be identified in the title, caption, in a table, or by separate notice. The order also prohibits AI-hallucinated citations or fictitious legal citations.\n\nMultiple courts have adopted a \"patchwork of rules and standing orders\" to address AI hallucination, with approaches ranging from complete bans to disclosure requirements to simple warnings about AI risks.\n\nThe search results include links to actual PDF documents of these standing orders from various federal district courts across the country."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"21683a2c-691a-4fe6-9596-b69c85d74e07"}

or (2) that any language drafted by generative artificial intelligence will be checked for accuracy by a human being.(no content)The Texas judge ordered attorneys to attest that they will not use ChatGPT or other generative artificial intelligence technology to write legal briefs because the AI tool can invent facts.\n\n**Eastern District of Texas:**\nOn October 31, 2025, Chief Judge Mazzant issued General Order 25-07, which revised, among other things, the court's Local Rule CV-11(g).(no content)The court recognized that it \"has seen an increasing number of filings that improperly utilized generative artificial intelligence by failing to review and verify the factual and legal accuracy of all content generated by such tools.\"\n\n**Southern District of Texas:**\nGeneral Order 2025-04 requires that an attorney or self-represented litigant certifies their claims, defenses, and other legal contentions are warranted by existing law, and cautioned against submitting to the Court any pleading drafted using generative artificial intelligence without checking the submission for accuracy as certain technologies may produce factually or legally inaccurate content.\n\n### Key Requirements\n\nMany Texas districts have \"Standing Orders\" on AI that require a signed \"AI Certification.\"(no content)One Texas court has required that all filers (including self-represented litigants) certify that \"all language, quotations, sources, citations, arguments, and legal analysis created or contributed to by generative artificial intelligence were before submission verified as accurate through traditional (non-AI) legal sources by an attorney licensed to practice law in the State of Texas.\""}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"ecd813ff-d2e6-4d1d-a9e0-159e9ff16d71"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Overview\nJudicial response to generative AI is evolving rapidly, with courts issuing a profusion of standing orders, pretrial orders, court rules, general orders, and case management orders imposing various obligations on litigants and counsel to certify the use of AI technology.\n\n## State Court Examples\n\n**North Carolina:**\n- North Carolina's Supreme Court has not adopted a rule or policy governing the use of AI, leaving the matter to be addressed by individual judicial districts. Only one trial court district in the state, Superior Court District 25 (Cabarrus County), has adopted an administrative order addressing AI.\n\n**Illinois:**\n- The Illinois Supreme Court adopted a policy on artificial intelligence that fell into the no-disclosure-but-be-careful camp, stating that attorneys, judges, and self-represented litigants are accountable for their final work product.\n- Illinois state courts are currently disclosure-optional (with an inclination against requiring it).\n\n**General State Court Landscape:**\n- Despite growing concern, there is not (yet) any single, district, or state-wide local rule on disclosing the use of AI in court filings.\n- There's no nationwide rule. Whether you must disclose AI use depends on the judge or court, plus your ongoing duties of competence, confidentiality, supervision, and candor.\n\n## Tracking Resources\nSeveral trackers are available to monitor these rules:\n- The Ropes & Gray Court Order Tracker (publicly available via a map) or, for paid Lexis subscribers, the Lexis Federal and State Court Rules Tracker which includes summaries of U.S. court orders, local rules, and guidelines."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"dd515642-073c-4802-ac9f-ed990b3f5207"}

In the past several weeks, chief judges in Miami-Dade and Broward counties issued AI-related administrative orders governing the 11th and 17th judicial circuits, respectively.\n\n### 11th Judicial Circuit (Miami-Dade County)\n\nEleventh Judicial Circuit Chief Judge Ariana Fajardo Orshan issued Administrative Order No. 26-04 \"Re: Disclosure of Use of Generative Artificial Intelligence by Attorneys and Self-Represented Litigants,\" on January 15.(no content)Pleadings filed in the Eleventh Judicial Circuit must now carry the following AI certification: \"Generative artificial intelligence was used in the preparation of this filing. The undersigned certifies that all factual assertions, legal authority, and citations have been independently reviewed and verified for accuracy and accepts full responsibility for the contents of this filing.\"\n\n### 17th Judicial Circuit (Broward County)\n\nSeventeenth Judicial Circuit Chief Judge Carol-Lisa Phillips issued Administrative Order 2026-03-Gen, \"Use of Artificial Intelligence in Court Filings,\" on January 26.(no content)The order requires that all AI-generated content must be reviewed for accuracy, and include certification stating: \"The undersigned hereby certifies that generative artificial intelligence was used to prepare this [TITLE OF DOCUMENT BEING FILED]. The undersigned has independently verified the accuracy of every citation to the law and /or the record, and the accuracy of any language drafted by generative artificial intelligence, including quotations, citations, paraphrased assertions, facts, and legal analysis.\"\n\n### 6th Judicial Circuit\n\nA standing order from Section 22 of the Family Court Division applies to all attorneys appearing before the Court on behalf of clients and all self-represented litigants who submit filings, motions, briefs, memoranda, proposed orders, affidavits, or any other material. Here's what I discovered:\n\nDistrict Judge Margaret I. Strickland issued a standing order establishing disclosure and certification requirements for generative artificial intelligence. The order requires that when AI is used, the specific AI tool must be identified, and the unrepresented party or attorney must certify that they have checked the accuracy of any AI-generated portion.\n\nThis standing order appears to have been implemented in response to ongoing issues with AI-generated content in legal filings. Courts in New Mexico have detected AI-generated hallucinations in at least seven lawsuits since 2023, sometimes imposing sanctions but more often issuing warnings.\n\nThe full standing order document is available as a PDF from the U.S. District Court for the District of New Mexico's official website at: https://www.nmd.uscourts.gov/sites/nmd/files/Standing%20Order%20Regarding%20Use%20of%20Generative%20AI.pdf"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"81ef059e-d32b-407a-bde4-21717a4f6657"}

results, here's what I found about the Eastern District of Texas AI local rule amendment in 2025:\n\n## Key Information\n\nOn November 3, 2025, Judge Mazzant issued General Order 25-07 amending the EDTX local rules, with the revised rule set to take effect on December 1, 2025, after a public notice and comment period.\n\n## The Amendment\n\nThe revised CV-11(g) will require \"all litigants,\" not just pro se litigants, to review and verify the accuracy and quality of information produced by AI technology that is used in a filing. This is a significant expansion from the original 2023 rule that only applied to pro se litigants.\n\n## Reasoning\n\nThe court has seen an increasing number of filings that improperly utilized generative artificial intelligence by failing to review and verify the factual and legal accuracy of all content generated by such tools. The court emphasized that the obligations of Fed. R. Civ. P. 11, and other similar legal and ethical requirements, apply regardless of how a filing is prepared, and violations of these obligations can result in sanctions.\n\nThe amendment reflects the court's recognition that licensed attorneys are now also relying on AI technology in their practice, necessitating broader requirements for verification of AI-generated content."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"06cb9514-aeff-46c9-9aa3-5d98f6507af2"}

Here's what I found:\n\n## General Order 2025-04\n\nThe Southern District of Texas issued General Order 2025-04, which explains that attorneys must exercise independent legal judgment and thoroughly review any filing prepared with the assistance of generative AI before submission.(no content)The order was signed on May 7, 2025.\n\nThe order has already been applied in enforcement actions. A court in the district identified multiple citations that appeared fictitious or materially inaccurate, and determined that these inaccurate case citations, which stemmed from the use of generative AI, constituted a Rule 11 violation.(no content)The court held that an attorney's delegation to a law clerk who used AI unsupervised did not excuse the failure to verify the cited authorities.\n\n## Key Requirements\n\nThe order emphasizes that attorneys have a non-delegable responsibility to verify all content in their filings, regardless of whether generative AI was used in preparation. The order works in conjunction with Federal Rule of Civil Procedure 11 to ensure the accuracy of court filings.\n\nYou can access the full order directly at the official court website: https://www.txs.uscourts.gov/sites/txs/files/general-orders/General_Order_2025-04_Use_of_Generative_AI_in_Court_Filings.pdf"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"0ea33274-a196-4a77-ba11-7d840a81e6c2"}

Here's what I discovered:\n\n## California Judicial Council AI Disclosure Requirement\n\nOn July 18, 2025, the Judicial Council of California unanimously adopted the first statewide framework in the nation governing the use of generative artificial intelligence within the judicial branch.(no content)Rule 10.430 adopted effective September 1, 2025.\n\n### Key Disclosure Requirements\n\nThe policy requires disclosure of the use of or reliance on generative AI if the final version of a written, visual, or audio work provided to the public consists entirely of generative AI outputs. Disclosure must be made through a clear and understandable label, watermark, or statement that describes how generative AI was used and identifies the system used.\n\n### Policy Adoption Deadline\n\nAny court that does not prohibit the use of generative AI by court staff or judicial officers must adopt a generative AI use policy by December 15, 2025.(no content)This rule applies to the superior courts, the Courts of Appeal, and the Supreme Court.\n\n### Other Key Provisions\n\nThe policies must also:\n- Prohibit entry of confidential or nonpublic information into public AI systems, require accuracy verification and correction of hallucinated outputs\n- Bar AI use that unlawfully discriminates or disparately impacts protected groups\n- Require meaningful human review and correction of AI-generated content(no content)California is the largest, and one of the first, court systems in the United States to adopt such a framework."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"510eb65b-5529-4262-a71b-8f8631022bb7"}

Here's what I found:\n\nIn June 2024, the U.S. District Court for the Western District of North Carolina issued a standing order requiring lawyers to file a certification alongside every brief stating that AI was not used to help prepare the brief.\n\nMore specifically, briefs or memoranda submitted to the court must have a certification that no artificial intelligence was used during the research to prepare the document except for that in the standard online legal research sources Bloomberg, FastCase, Lexis or Westlaw. Additionally, either an attorney or a paralegal working at his or her direction has verified every statement and citation for accuracy.(no content)Federal judges in the Western District of North Carolina, including Charlotte, have issued an order limiting the use of artificial intelligence platforms in filing legal briefs and memorandums.\n\nThe order was issued in response to concerns about reliability and accuracy, with the court noting that briefs prepared using AI platforms like ChatGPT have increased the court's concern regarding the reliability and accuracy of filings, and there have been several reports around the country regarding courts receiving briefs containing fictitious case cites and unsupported arguments generated by AI.\n\nThe actual PDF of the standing order is available at the court's official website (ncwd.uscourts.gov)."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4be82ee4-a2a8-4604-b12e-8495b1726cbf","uuid":"c9308239-6470-4c0a-a761-bb4459534fdf"}
